RATING: 2
2nd play (Marcy Case), 3er
Yup, this game is not for me: 4-->2

---
1 play, 4er

This is a board game version of those old adventure style computer games (like Myst, Zork): you explore the environment, interacting with people/scenery trying to collect clues in order to solve the big puzzle (likely with little puzzles along the way). This time there's a time travel theme, so you can go through the game repeatedly having a bit more information each time -- just like restarting those old computer games. Of course, if you need some key pieces of equipment, or if you just can't remember exactly what you saw where, you'll end up doing the same things repeatedly. 

Unfortunately for me, I HATE those puzzley computer games! I hate having to memorize everything I do along the way, having to do it over and over again, having to work out all those annoying puzzles, having options presented to me with no basis given for which option is the "right" or better choice, getting smacked around for it ... HATE it!

I'd try it once again, if only in the hope that it will be better having actually learned the rules (why in the hell couldn't the rule book have explained things better?); and the fact that we bought the first expansion (Marcy case) as well as the base game. But I do not have high hopes.

AND WHY THE HELL IS THERE NO PERIOD AFTER THE E IN T.I.M.E ??!!?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
There are some neat storytelling ideas in this game, but for my tastes it relies too much on random factors. Non-mitigatable dice rolls, no clues as to what rooms you should be searching / ignoring, etc. Plus, I'm not keen on re-playing a scenario multiple times & having to rely on knowledge of previous run throughs. 

It's also not balanced for less than 4 players. For example, in scenario 1 where you get 25 time units theoretically (ignoring time loss due to movement) in a 4 player game you have a total of 100 actions to split amongst players. In a 3 player game that reduces to 75. The manual recommends starting a 30 time units, but you're still down by 20. Makes a big difference if you're not keen on multiple play-throughs -- with 20 time units you can potentially explore up to 3 rooms in full.

RATING: 8.5
2-4, 60-90m
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I was very resistant to buying this game.  A 40-50 dollar price tag for a single play (and additional plays available at 30 bucks a pop) was just too much.  I'm not against the idea of "consumable" games.  I'm currently in the middle of a Pandemic: Legacy playthrough and finished a Risk: Legacy playthrough and look forward to Seafall.  To a certain extent games like Descent and Pathfinder Adventure Card Game, which I have also played, are also consumable where while you CAN play them again it would be far less interesting after you have once (and for that matter there is nothing stopping you from playing through Pandemic or Risk: Legacy after a campaign ends either).  This game (or more specifically the included scenario) absolutely cannot be played through again after playing it once.  It is an adventure/mystery game and knowing the answers prevents you from playing it honestly again.

Sure, one "play" can be 2-3 sessions taking up to 5 hours but I have other games that take that long for a single play.  I have also heard the argument that many people have games they've spent this much for that they haven't played more than once or at all.  I do indeed have games I have only played once or not at all yet, but if I still own it I INTEND to play it again and I CAN.  I have also heard it was a good game so I was at least interested even if I wouldn't spend my own money for it, and to that end I actually won it in a G4GG auction which while not necessarily free, I earned my geek gold by giving away games of my own, is an investment I was at least comfortable with.

Okay, end of rant, onto the game.  Like I said, it is an adventure/mystery solving game.  The players are time travelers from the future jumping into the bodies of people in the past Quantum Leap-style to prevent some time destroying disaster from happening.  You do this by exploring areas and rolling dice to conquer challenges and find clues until some end game condition happens.  This usually takes multiple "runs" so you return to changed locations over and over again with new information and items until you reach the end of the adventure.

Honest-to-god, with a completely open mind, I just DO NOT get the love for this game from the board game community.  Completely taking the restrictive pricing model out of the picture, I now own a copy of this, and while it is a fine game it is nothing special.  You go from location to location, finding items to unlock new locations, and rolling dice corresponding to character stats to pass challenges like so many other coops out there.  Granted, this game has a much more cohesive story than something like Eldritch Horror or Robinson Crusoe, but the gameplay is actually less than those other games.

Even if you are really into story over substance though, and I can get that, a really good interactive story can elevate a game with less exciting gameplay, the story isn't THAT good.  This is with the caveat that I haven't finished the Asylum scenario yet, maybe there are some marquee moments and twists coming that will really change my mind.  But I'm 2 hours and 1 session into the game, which by all accounts is on average about halfway through and nothing has really impressed me yet.  In fact, sometimes quite the opposite.  There are often decisions, sometimes major ones, you as a player can make with no information to support them.  You really just randomly guess and deal with the consequences.

One thing I will say though is the game does look great.  The stark and clean base game components which are meant to act as a framework for any scenario look amazing against the beautiful watercolor cards that make up the location panoramas.  I also really do love how the box is designed to "save" your place in the game if you have to stop in the middle of a session.  Past that though everything in this game from story to content to actual gameplay is incredibly average.  Hearing nothing but accolades for this game I wasn't convinced to support the ridiculous pricing structure of this game.  Having played it and found it incredibly mediocre really baffles me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Really fun game, but so little content for the price.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I really like T.I.M.E Stories, but any praise must be prefaced with the obvious: it slices my soul like razor blades dipped in cheap mojito mixer that there isn't a period/full stop after the "E" in "T.I.M.E". 

I can't find any explanation for the lack of dot. Surely it's not like it was left off in a reflexive act of symmetry, a la when people write "R.I.P" - that's twitchily bothersome, but at least you can tell that they got caught up in decorating the "I". 

Something as slick as TIME Stories (I'm just going to go with period-less acronym style) shouldn't have such a basic error that points to the end times of civilization. (I'm a descriptivist when it comes to language use, I swear, but...) I hope that future reprints will fix this, even if it means that for some of us the base game and shrinkwrapped expansions won't match. (The Social Punctuation Contract trumps OCD!) That, or come up with a cool story to explain the missing full stop. 

About those shrinkwrapped expansions... they're still doing the Laura Palmer trick because my husband and I have yet to solve the base game. After getting stuck and turning to the internet for a tiny-tiny bit of clarification (fools that we are), we accidentally got a teeny spoiler (grr, forums without text-hiding tools...), so if all is lost we probably know how to continue, but of course it would be more fun to do things ourselves. However, now so much time has passed since we marathoned the base game that the thought of starting again and refamiliarizing ourselves with the game and what we already know is daunting. (New rule: take notes.)

I know that some people don't like controlling multiple characters in the two-player variant. Considering how much I hate doing this in Mysterium, it surprisingly doesn't bother me here. I guess the body hopping just fits the technology for me.

Something I don't like is the "oh, you failed a couple of times, now just take all the time you want, whatevers..." element. I understand the reasoning, but it seems to happen a little soon (or maybe we just haz dumbs), and it killed some of the immersion for us. We decided that the next time we played (which still has yet to happen), we'd still impose some time restrictions on ourselves. We're not settling for participation ribbons just yet!

I am so impressed with the originality of the "decksploration" concept, and I'm genuinely very excited for that time when we finish the base game (or at least pass our current hurdle - no idea how much is left after that) and can play the expansions (is that really the right word?) then eagerly get to await all of the new releases. 

So, I don't know if the "7.9" rating is quite accurate from me. TIME Stories was making me really happy, but the fact is that it hasn't come back to the table, and I have a list of other games that I want to play or play again before it does. Future Me will probably (hopefully) come back to re-rate this and scoff at this period of my life while envying me of my time-traveling adventures yet to come.

UPDATE: Played and played some more. Scoffed at Past Me. Even if I dislike all future expansions as much as I dislike Prophecy of Dragons, this is such a clever and fun game.

RATING: 5
The components are decent, but the artwork is beautiful and the storyline is pretty good. It keeps you interested throughout and the gradual revelation of the overarching mystery is really well done. The only real problem I have is that it's easy to get yourself into a position where it feels like the game is unwinnable because one of the puzzles requires some esoteric outside the box thinking, especially if you take a specific route through the game. It's not, but it requires some long, hard perusal of the items you do have in order to play properly. Original Rating: 8/10

EDIT: I played again with another group of mine because three of them wanted to play and they needed a fourth. I was interested in testing the replayability of the game, as there had been about two months between scenarios. Unfortunately, the game was either too fresh in my mind or the main puzzle to solve was just too memorable, as the experience was dreadfully boring. I tried to pretend that I didn't know what was coming, but as we entered each new location I remembered exactly what would happen and where. I was a good soldier and mostly tried not to guide the team too much down the correct path, but the experience was torturous just due to how games like this work. 

If you've ever played an old NES game, you'll know what I mean. Those games usually required a ton of trial and error or pattern memorization, but once you know the solution, you can comfortably complete them in fifteen minutes. Ultimately, this is true of T.I.M.E Stories as well, leading to the game feeling extremely shallow. It was hard sitting there going the wrong way numerous times when I wanted to just get to the main puzzle and beat the game already. It really is a one and done experience. My rating for replay is 2, so I'll just take the average and give the game a 5/10.

RATING: 9
This is a very good game.  It's major draw back is replayability.  You can keep playing an adventure until you solve it and then must move on to an expansion adventure.  That means the price per hour of entertainment you get out of it is high for a board game.  However, the actual entertainment is very good.

The game is essentially chose your own adventure turned into a board game, but is done very well.  It is also beautifully produced and has a look and feel which is very different for board games.  It is also highly thematic with a very simple rule set.  It also is compatible with some light role playing although the game overhypes this aspect.

So an excellent game, but expensive for the time involved.  However, I have a rich friend who owns it, so that's ideal.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Another evolution of the gamebook, T.I.M.E Stories uses the conceit of a "12 Monkeys" meets "Quantum Leap" agency: players travel in time or to alternate dimensions, inhabiting bodies of characters there, to collectively solve mysteries represented by decks of cards.

Decks consist primarily of locations, cards combined to produce a panoramic picture. Each card in a location is also a specific element to be investigated (talk to the young man in the corner, look at the painting on the wall etc.), usually resulting in either information, a test (such as a fight, via dice rolling) or an item. Some locations are locked without specific triggers, such as already having visited another area or having an item. All of this costs time units; once the team runs out they are whisked back to the T.I.M.E Agency HQ and will need to go back in for another run, this time forearmed with knowledge from previous runs.

In The Asylum, the only case included in the base set, this works much the way you would expect. Completing the case requires solving a single large puzzle requiring information from areas so spread out as to be very difficult to obtain by accident in a single run. There are several traps, one major dead end and a secret.

It essentially plays out like a point-and-click adventure game, in the Sierra/LucasArts/etc. tradition, in board form. It's better than its gamebook antecedents in a number of ways, most notably the lavish illustrations and the map mechanism that, as with the combination map and and directory in Sherlock Holmes: Consulting Detective, gives players real agency in choosing where to go and what to do.

Enjoyable as it is, though, it's assuredly not perfect.

Some elements of The Asylum, and I assume later scenarios, are frustrating because of the game's not entirely successful combination of roleplaying and puzzle. A number of trap cards, for example, resulting in useless combat or other delays, were easily avoidable, entirely predictable consequences of obviously poor decisions. In-universe, punching a policeman in the face seems like a bad idea; but as a game with limited choices, if we players have been given the opportunity then should we give it a go? I imagine that to get the most out of the experience, you really need to play as though every action is precious and not chase down every single lead. But since the story is more interesting than the mechanisms, and the entire conceit of the game is that multiple runs are required, the time pressure isn't really felt.

Similarly, random elements like combat are quite enjoyable and menacing with the energy of the mystery to sustain interest, but irritating afterwards -- we died on the final Asylum boss, for example, and pretended we didn't because it was all in a series of spectacularly terrible rolls (e.g. 1 damage from 9 dice). Why bother doing another run?

I'm also hopeful that some future puzzles may be a little more organic. A big part of the charm of Sherlock Holmes: Consulting Detective (and similar games like Gumshoe) is that the mysteries are based on clues like inconsistencies in witness testimony. There's a real worry that the mechanical trappings of T.I.M.E Stories will force nothing but clumsy letter/number based puzzles.

Still, I liked it enough that a few more scenarios are on the way.

Rating incorporates play of case 1, The Asylum, only.

RATING: 4
If you can borrow a copy, play a friend's copy, or play this at a con, by all means go for it. The very limited replay value is, IMHO, quite a big hit against it though.

This is a clever adaption of point-and-click adventure games to board game format. I'm a big fan of P&C games, so I find that kind of cool, but it does make me wonder: is there really any NEED to turn these into a boardgame? This probably would have been better as a computer game.

The game makes clever use of cards to have a repeatable plotline, and includes neat features like showing a panorama scene composed of multiple cards for characters to interact with. Playing the game is fun and sucks you into trying to figure out what is going on and to solve the mystery.

However, the plot itself is quite weak, and very inconsistent in genre and tone (sometimes in really tacky ways). Don't expect to actually have things make much sense.

The overall setup of repeating loops is a neat concept, but the specific implementation leaves a lot to be desired, crushing player input in favor of heavy-handed external controls. This could have handled much better with some simple tweaks and flavor changes.
+co-op+

RATING: 6.8
tl;dr: interesting, but unsure how much I like it

Deduction and co-op are not my favorite elements in games, so putting them together doesn't sound very appealing. But of course, when a game gets as much attention as this one gets, I want to try it.
So this is a scenario based game, and you walk around from location to location, gathering items and clues to solve a mystery. Everyone has a character with some skills that can be quite different. That's more akin to your run-of-the-mill RPG title, also not my favorite. But it works pretty well and it's all quite streamlined.
We had to play the scenario 3 times to get through it. Each play took about 1 hour. 1 hour is fine. 3 hours is not so fine. And there are ways to save your game between runs, but you don't want to do that because then you will have to take a lot of notes, and that's no fun, is it? So, that's a bit annoying but 3 hours is still OK if you plan ahead.
So I found it fairly enjoyable but I had a couple of issues with it. The main one being that this is not really my style. But I recognize the quality of the design.
Which brings me to my main gripe with the game. This game is sold for the price of a big box game. It is scenario based. The scenarios cannot be replayed. The box comes with only one scenario. ONE! That is a ripoff. Now you have to fork out €20 or €25 each and every time you want to play this game again. That is just shameful! Games with scenarios that you cannot replay should include 10 scenarios or more. I don't fork out money for a game to play it once. So I won't be forking out money for this game and wouldn't recommend anyone do so.

Initial rating: 6.8/10 (for the game play, not the package)
BGG scale: 6/10

RATING: 2

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I was really intrigued by this game, as it was strongly recommended by a friend. However, after playing the base adventure, I was left with a sour taste in my mouth. The game is structured like an "escape the room" sort of puzzle, framed as a mystery to solve. However, there's no consistent logic to any choices you may make.
Early on we learned that interacting with other characters may net us items or clues for later use, or they may cause encounters that could harm us or waste precious "time" (the game has a "beat the clock" mechanic to keep things moving).
However, choosing where to go and who to talk to is often purely a guessing game. Certain conversations or items may be clues as to where to go next, but most of the time you walk into a room and just head towards what looks interesting. And even those may be misleading. This is a slight hyperbole, but if you enter an area to find a man in one corner mumbling, "I'mma cut ya..", talking to him has just as much a chance to get stabbed as it does to get a free knife that you need for a later puzzle. Since it's all a guessing game, the time limit is the only real reason not to just explore and interact with everything -- and that seems to be the idea. Since the time limit doesn't end the game, but forces you to start all over again armed with new knowledge, there's less mystery-solving and more trial and error. And that gets very repetitive quickly. After our first rewind, I had already begun to see the seams tearing.
Overall, while the initial novelty is neat and worth a look, I can't really recommend T.I.M.E Stories. I've heard each expansion case is very different, and I am interested in trying again on a new story, but the base is entirely skippable and, if you're like me (I know I am), it may run the risk of almost turning you off entirely.

RATING: 5
The long-term success of the T.I.M.E. Stories system will hinge on the publisher selling follow-on story packs.   However, the experience is not immersive.  The card art is dwarfed by a giant white board, and the flavor text in the base is scanty.  Combat is primitive.  I'm not sure that the bland environment and reusable components will sustain gamers' interest--except in the near-certain development of fan fiction.  But if this works, T.I.M.E. Stories could be a large and lucrative franchise à la the Choose Your Own Adventure book series.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.5
This is a hard game to describe, in part because there really isn't a comparable tabletop game that I can think of... the closest analogues are probably video games like Resident Evil, or Nintendo DS puzzle games like Professor Layton or Phoenix Wright, mixed with the movie Groundhog Day.  And the TV show Quantum Leap ("Oh, boy...").  

Basically, the game comes with a single story scenario that players will play through, again and again, until they complete it within the time allotted.  Some clues you learn will become consistent changes in future tries at the scenario, though, so it's not like you're just playing the exact thing over and over.  The telling of the story evolves (or at least streamlines) to prevent becoming stale on subsequent attempts.

It really is incredibly unique and innovative, and full of great characters and loads of style.  Once you've finished a scenario, though, there's really no replayability, but future expansions providing all new scenarios are already in the works.

I do have two critiques, though.  One is that it's over-produced.  It has a superfluous board and seems to include more tokens than necessary.  For a game with such low replayability, and one that necessarily puts you on the expansion treadmill, it'd be nice if they made the package smaller and with a lower price tag.

My other critique is that it's an awkward and fairly artificial rule that explored cards cannot be read aloud to members of the group who occupy other spaces in the same location, but can relay the gist of the content.  I see no purpose to this rule.

I guess I have a third and fourth critique in that its replay value is incredibly limited, especially if you happen to sprint through the scenario.  But I don't personally own the game, so haven't paid the over-inflated price, so critiques about replayability and value don't apply to me so much.

UPDATE: The more I play, the more I'm wanting to smash the clock radio to stop the Sonny and Cher...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I like the Groundhog day/Edge of tomorrow-mechanic this game is using. I like the way this game combines board and role playing games. It really captures some of the mood from the good old days in the dungeon (when there was time for days). But there is one big question that spoiled gameplay a bit. Do you play with the mindset of the host your time agent occupies or do you play with the mindset of the time agent from the future? I tried to play schizophrenically. With both mindsets being stuck in one body. The girl prone to panic attacks, clinging to anyone with her, still with the time agent around knowing that he's here on a mission and trying to focus on that. But this is a very hard thing to keep up and the atmosphere of the game mostly depends on the willingness and the talent of your group to get the inner actor to the surface. That's why I rate it as a 7 "only".

RATING: 6
I'm not completely sure how to rate this. The scenario that comes with the base set, Asylum, is just okay. I might give  a 5 to that scenario alone. However, the first expansion scenario, The Marcy Case, is excellent. Even though The Marcy Case came out at the same time as the base game, I imagine it was actually developed after the base scenario. It feels like they improved on almost everything that was wrong with Asylum.

About the actual game: it's a pretty clever choose-your-own adventure game. The mechanics are simple enough to let players stay focused on the adventure. You move around from location to location, have encounters that mainly have you test your stats by rolling dice (Arkham/Eldritch Horror style), and try to solve the case before running out of time.

My only real issue is with the price. I didn't mind paying $50 for Pandemic Legacy because I got about 18 hours of gameplay out of it, and it was an awesome experience. Even though it can't be replayed, the price seemed in line with the experience and amount of time. With TIME Stories, it seems like each case can take around 2.5-4.5 hours and they really can't be replayed once completed. At $20 per expansion case, this seems excessive and I'm not sure that it will be worth keeping for that price.

And just for the record, on the Pandemic Legacy vs TIME Stories debate, I'm definitely on the side of PL.

Overall a fun experience but I'm just not sure if it will be worth the cost in the long run.

RATING: 6
Full of interesting ideas and brimming with surprises, marred by piss-poor actual writing. This is not a problem unique to T.I.M.E. Stories - many board games struggle with plot and character development - but in a game where storytelling is so critical to the core pitch, it's a killer. The expansions that I've played (The Marcy Case and Lumen Fidei) don't get any better in this regard. It's a lot of cliches followed by a resounding "well, I guess it's over?"

To its credit, the puzzle-solving elements of T.I.M.E. Stories can be exceptionally clever, and the base game does this best. It's just a shame it ultimately feels like a third-rate adventure game.

RATING: 7

Some cards stay in place but most are reset so you can get a second chance at the adventure with the added bonus of knowing where to/not to go/do. I'm going to give it  a few weeks before my next attempt. A great solo experience.

RATING: 4
First Impression after 4 runs:

It's less a boardgame and more an experience. Something like a (barely) interactive movie. The time track and the resets of the runs annoyed me to a great level and felt forced to make the game last longer than it should. The game can't be lost, unless you choose to, so the whole combat levels also felt ridiculously out of place. The riddles where surprisingly few, to the point of frustration. Whatever cooperation might have been required from the storytelling description I completely missed it. No meaningfull decisions for anyone playing this game. After the first couple of runs even the set up of the rooms was a tiring and tedious procedure. We didn't for example ever set up the first after the first time, we mentally travelled from it to save time.

The only thing that felt right was the artwork and the basic receptables' characters that were still completely disconnected to the rest of the story.

Overall, the first story fell flat for me, and left me wanting. Just repeating it feels completely pointless for me, even with a new group, as it wasn't that involving to create different reactions to different players. So it's a 1, normally, either from a boardgame or an rpg percpective.

The 4 is there for the other stories, as I can see this game as a decent way to tell a story to a group of people and get some nice rp reactions from them, but the story needs to be really good and the time track really thrown away to save time to add to the story. I'm willing to give this another go, even if based on the first story I have little hopes for it. 

In the end, I can't really call this a game, it feels completely out of place ranked and filed here in BGG as one just because it comes in a box. A game, any game, needs to have a goal and this one doesn't - other than to end it.

RATING: 9
The system itself gets a straight 10 from me. It's innovativ, fun and easy to learn. The level of immersion is great!

The "Asylum" case however left us a bit underwhelmed. It only gets a 7 from me. (See below for more). I really hope other cases are stronger, the system deserves it!


----SPOILER WARNING!!!!-----

Our biggest problem with the asylum case was, that the solution lies in simply going everywhere and pick up everything that you can. Many of the Pentagrams where just conveniently lying around somewhere where they had no business of being. We felt reminded of many of the weaker point and click adventures in computer games. There where no decisions on "where to go next" that could be based on anything. You just had to try everything and only repeat meaningful stuff next time.
Seccond problem: We had a very frustrating stretch, where we went into the Catacombs (lots of fighting). Then stand before the final gate without all Pentagrams (no way of solving) so we went on through the only other way out (lots more fighting, one of us dying) only to end up at a dead end with about 15 time wasted on absolutely nothing...
Comme on guys! You don't do that to players! At least let us find something interesting at the end of this. No Role-play master would let his players suffer this hard. The mood at the table was at an absolute low point, especially since it was the 3. run, so we could not just let it end and try again. We all knew we just screwed our scoring significantly. We ended up having 7 Points which is probably alright. The mood lightened in the second half of the run, but we never really came back from this.

------END OF SPOILERS------

RATING: 8

[+] interesting variety of receptacle, but...
[-] unclear objective and confusing rule for 3rd run; too many "dead ends" that you can not avoid; you pretty much have to "try" every locations, so in the end there is only one path to victory; the puzzle sucks! the setting is cool but the mystical/paranormal creatures come out of the blue

=> increased rating to 8


UP: very disappointed by Expedition: Endurance (the worst scenario), a useless first run, a single path to follow, obvious false leads, an uninspired super-easy end scene, overall a weird theme


UP: pleasantly surprised by the level of overarching story-telling from Lumen Fidei, the scenario has a few very good moments; really looking forward to the implications of our "result" over the next scenarios

UP: loved the 3-tiered nature of Estrella drive, as well as the more adult content; I still wished there were implications from the previous mission

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
T.I.M.E. Stories is the cardboard equivalent of an 80's era point-and-click PC adventure game. This is a story that's completely on rails, the gameplay simply being multiple passes at turn left or turn right type decisions until you find the right path or are lucky enough to roll the right dice to proceed.

That's not to say that it is not a neat experience. It follows the format of most adventure games, with a paragraph of text being read as the players enter each location or flip a surprise event. In a nice flourish of immersion, the text that is being read is also accompanied by an interesting illustration of the scene, painted mural style across the back of the other cards players are about to interact with at that location. It really pulls you into the story and makes planning the team's actions a much more interactive experience as the players debate the risk and rewards of interacting with the dangers presented in the artwork.

While the game pulls off all of the best parts of those point-and-clicks, it also suffers from some of the less interesting parts of those games. Exploration is great, but when you have to replay a scene it becomes a largely uninteresting exercise of retracing and repeating puzzles you've already solved. There is also zero replayability once you've completed an adventure since they are essentially a simple maze of encounters where the correct path is nigh impossible to forget, once you've found the answer.

If this were a 20 or 30-hour game, this would be much less of a concern. Unfortunately, it only takes 2-4 hours to run through the base scenario which isn't a whole lot of bang for your $50 buy-in. The expansions are significantly less expensive, but again, even at $25 or so, I'm not sure the value is there for the single session of play it delivers. Honestly, I'm not even sure the money is as much of a factor for me as the shelf space. Once you've collected a half dozen scenarios, this thing is taking up a whole shelf and you are storing things you can never use again!

This is probably a good investment for someone that has both a lot of traffic through their game room and an urge to watch other people play their game since they can no longer play it themselves. That certainly isn't me, so i'll happily play some more adventures, but I certainly won't be buying into the system.

RATING: 7
I'm a prime target for TIME Stories, in that I loved the Infocom interactive fiction games in my youth - to the point where I [url=http://ifdb.tads.org/search?searchfor=author%3ACody+Sandifer]wrote a few decently-reviewed IF games myself[/url] in the late 90s.

Do old-timey text adventures still hold up today?  Sometimes.  Adventures with intricately timed puzzles were the most frustrating to play, and TIME Stories is exactly that type of game.  (So was my own [url=http://ifdb.tads.org/viewgame?id=nzydrfu1rl2qkuop]Zero Sum Game[/url], and ironically if I could reverse time and change that aspect of ZSG I probably would.)  But time-limited puzzles did pose an interesting challenge, and they were always satisfying to beat.  Or not, and you threw up your hands in disgust and stopped playing.

One issue particular to TIME Stories is that the game is meant to be played exactly once, so everything about it needs to be perfect: rules, mechanics, presentation, all-around fairness, and internal consistency.  (By the latter, I mean that, if I'm not allowed to search a bush in one room, I shouldn't need to search a bush in another room to solve a puzzle - that wouldn't be consistent, since based on prior experience I would assume that wouldn't be possible.)

It's not clear to me that the central puzzle in TIME Stories is fair. Not because it isn't properly clued, but because the interface itself is wonky.  We were baffled at one point because (a) we didn't know if we had sufficient information to solve the puzzle (we did, it turned out) and (b) it was unclear how we would know if the puzzle was even solved. I think that's a problem that should have been addressed in playtesting, though I'm not sure how.  Unfortunately, this means the game fails the 'perfection' test, and it does so in such a way that it can potentially be ruined - or at least repeated to the point of frustration - unless you decide to hunt down a spoiler-free FAQ (which shouldn't be necessary).

Finally, the one-and-done play model is financially annoying in the extreme.  You either need a sugar daddy with cash to burn, or all players bite the bullet and chip in a few bucks for the privilege of playing.  Then perhaps you pay the game forward and let some other group enjoy the experience.

In my case, I'm glad that I played someone else's copy, and I would be happy to try others in the series.  There's no way I would fork out the cash myself, though.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Not all the rules are explained properly. The game itself is not that amazing as I thought it would be. The high rating comes from the experience that I had with my friends. I guess the game did deliver nice amount of fun and interest (for the very short time though), enough for me to rate it 8. Although, at the same time, it kind of left me with the feeling of disappointment. I was expecting something else and I think the brilliant idea of the game wasn't executed in the best way it could. 
The puzzles were interesting but rather easy and not enough of them. I guess the game has to appeal to larger audience so they can't be too hard.
I think the scenario should be way longer and the time progression should be a little more lenient. I feel like they were too lazy to make the game longer so instead of that, they made the time token progress extremely fast so that it would be impossible to get to the end on your 1st or 2nd try (unless we were moving the time token too much because of the badly written instruction manual; we ran out of time after only a few locations with not throughout exploration) unless you know the exact, shortest path to victory. So what we ended up with is a short game with a little bit stressful (in negative way) rush coupled with artificial optimization of your moves with each consecutive play. (Your final playthrough of the game is going to be dull, almost giving you the feeling of setting up a game rather than playing it)

In an essance it's like a worse version of PC RPG game, however, with an advantage that you can play it with your friends over the table with the best possible interaction and cooperation.
Also, the story of the first scenario is really flat. The game doesn't give you any details of the story (lazily telling you that it is your job to discover what is going on - although I guess it's ok). It starts mysterious and intriguing but suddenly the plot becomes really weird and shallow. The ending was really disappointing.

Overall, worth playing, great experience, but the idea wasn't executed too well. The game is extremely luck dependent, majorly flawed, and heavily overpriced.

RATING: 10
My first play of The Asylum mission in the base game of T.I.M.E Stories is definitely one of my most enjoyable, thrilling, and gripping game sessions of my life. This is a game custom made for me. I don't really care that you can only really play each case once, because to me it was well worth the investment. If every game session was as much fun as this, then I'd easily plunk down $50 each game night (I sort of do that already though).

I've been a roleplayer for over 30 years, but sadly very rarely in the role of a player character, instead taking on the role of a gamemaster. T.I.M.E Stories feels like a beautiful, and perfect marriage of an RPG, and a cooperative board game. The deck in this "decksploration" game acts as the gamemaster, and the players very much have to cooperate to figure out the enigma, and successfully complete their mission.

The theme of time travel is one I adore, and it's such a smart choice for this game, as you can play the same time traveler, you, each time, but in another receptacle. Fantastic!

If you're a board gamer, or table top RPG player you owe it to yourself to experience T.I.M.E Stories.

RATING: 9
I don't know how to rate TIME Stories.  A 10 was tempting based on the high points... but we were ready to throw it out the window in frustration at another point so this seems about right.

The theme being sort of like Quantum Leap but on a team of Navy Seals is neat.  Playing the game feels a lot like a Choose Your Own Adventure book where you are trying to find one specific ending... or a bit like the computer game Myst with a lot more violence.  

I think I like the first time into a mission the best as everything is new and you are feeling out both the setting and the puzzle/mystery you are trying to solve.  Once you've gone through the first iteration or two you probably have a good idea what you need to accomplish and are trying to find the most efficient way to achieve your task.  This is interesting in its own right but a lot of the efficiency comes down to die rolls and being clever enough to solve the puzzle set before you.  The elation of solving that puzzle is pretty great... but feeling completely stumped (or needing to brute force a solution through multiple trips through time) can be frustratingly unfun.  

It is also fair to say this does not have the replay value of a typical game.  I think diving into each new mission will always be fun... but a mission you played is going to need to sit for 6 months... or a year... or more... before you play it again in hopes that you've forgotten the exact details to make the puzzle fresh again, just like reading a mystery book a second time.

For now I'm very much looking forward to the next mission packs and solving those new puzzles with my friends.

RATING: 5
Played through the scenario that comes in the game in around 3 hours. 

I love the mechanics and setting, and the way an adventure game is implemented in card form, but the game needed much better writing.

The game's ostensibly about time travel, but in practice it feels like exploring a setting that's got nothing to do with the sci-fi premise, on a time limit. The characters aren't explored in any great detail, and there's a lot of fluff around the idea of a deep mystery that doesn't really work out to anything meaningful.

It's frustrating to think that someone paid $50 for this. We figured that all the chits are multipurpose, so they can probably print an expansion that's just a deck of cards and charge $10 or so - but nope! The expansion is $30!
